Hi there!  Are you a member of APTA?  Why or why not? Thanks! 

(Full disclosure, I am on the MN APTA board and talking to national APTA to learn why people are not members and how APTA can support the independent PTs. I have always said APTA doesnâ€™t bring value to people like us but I wanted to hear from you). Thanks!

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Not any more. 

I had payed APTA dues and multiple section dues every year since I started PT school in 1997. I budgeted to pay  over $1000 a year to APTA to cover state and national dues, Ortho section, Sports Section, Private Practice section, and Womenâ€™s Health section when my employers didnâ€™t cover it. 

I attended numerous APTA annual conferences and CSM and paid for employees to attend those conferences too. 

I donated $$ to the Political Action Committee. 

I volunteered my time to serve on the House of Delegates in my state and assisted with State Chapter Task Force initiatives to fight to protect our ability to perform joint manipulation and dry needling. 

When I opened my own private practice in 2012 I paid in full for all my clinicians to have an APTA and state PT association membership and 1 section membership.

This year I made the decision for the first time in 25+ years to cancel my APTA membership -  their ongoing lack of support for those of us who are independent private practice clinicians and the true issues we are facing daily is utterly disappointing and I donâ€™t feel hopeful it will improve.

Over my career I have only seen and experienced;
*increasing administrative & documentation burdens
*Inadequate & declining reimbursement rates
*flat lining of salaries for experienced clinicians not commiserate to their skill, training, advanced certification 
* reduced PT insurance benefits for beneficiaries 
*increasing productivity demands due to falling reimbursement and rising operational costs 
* plummeting morale from those of us that have been in our profession for any time working thru these challenges and burdens
*lack of action from APTA to do anything about these PT mill clinics that unethically schedule/bill in clear violation of payer contracts

> **Response 2:** Thereâ€™s not much valueâ€¦the â€œadvocacyâ€ has not been a priority. And because it has not been a priority,PT is becoming an unsustainable profession. How do you keep serving your community when business costs keep rising and reimbursements keep dropping? The amount of education, continuing education, time and volume needed to succeed are not reflected in the what you bring home at the end of the day and itâ€™s almost like the APTA has never fully addressed this growing concern. And this is why so many of us have turned to cash based services which quite honestly makes the APTA largely irrelevant.

### Discussion 18: [HIGH ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2023-08-23 21:54:48 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 2 reactions, 71 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1304670576970838/

**Original Post:**
I have a client who's feeling really down about her TKR that she can't get full ROM out of. She's 4 months post op and current AROM is 6-103. Dr refuses to do a MUA bc he was worried he'd break her femur (she has extensive scar tissue from an accident thirty years ago). No other surgeon will touch it until she's a year post op. Right now we are continuing to stretch and stretch and stretch, but she's feeling frustrated and wanted to know how often this happens and what the outcomes are. I told her I'd poll the group bc I wasn't sure. Thank you for any input!!!

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** A few other considerations:
What was her ROM prior to TKR? In severe OA cases ROM can be quite compromised prior to Sx. Long term soft tissue contractures, muscle atrophy and fascial restrictions can take a long time to improve. 
Function is far more important than end ROM. 
Inflammation of local soft tissue and scarring can decrease over time. Patience is key. 
Pilates reformer exercises can work magic on TKRs.

> **Response 2:** My 2 cents:
1. How about focusing on function rather than pain. 
2. Her AROM is really good at 4 months, could also be that pain is inhibiting you from stretching further. Maybe she is over stretching and over doing her exercises. It is ok to decrease intensity and frequency to calm her pain down 
3. Icing is known to decrease C fiber activity. You can try stretch - ice massage- stretch- ice massage â€¦my experience with TKR, their knee tend to get very hot with movement 
4. Educate and re assure that she is progressing well - if patient sees that you are worried, she gets worried as well which will contribute to her pain experience 
5. Celebrate small wins

In my 7 years of practice, I have seen several TKR  cases from many years ago who were not able to achieve full ROM but they are not limited to what they can do

---

### Discussion 19: [HIGH ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2024-01-21 22:36:15 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 10 reactions, 63 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1397112427726652/

**Original Post:**
Just sharing with like minds. This is my grandmas knee X-ray (permission to post). She has her TKA scheduled Tuesday this week. What the HECK!! Calcified quad tendon? Her joint surfaces actually look pretty good, right? I donâ€™t read X-ray a lot but dang. How did this happen lol. And/or what the heck is this going on here.

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** i'm sure that a strength deficit from a dysfunctional quad tendon can make her buckle easily  and fall. How old is she, is she walking with a device. You can most certainly delay the surgery while you get a 2nd and 3rd opinion; A quad tendon repair will be no fun but more appropriate than a TKR.

> **Response 2:** There are clear osteophytes and a decrease in joint space. That looks like a clear case of knee OA to me. Surgeons always tell patients that they're the ones to say when it's time for surgery. So if she's in pain and it's limiting her day to day function, do the TKA.

**Original Post:**
What is the general view of using manual muscle tests as an objective measure of progress? Our clinic is trying to steer few of us veterans away from using manual muscle tests and it feels like they just don't want to go through the hassle of doing them. I feel like they are really easy to do and can be measurable goals. Please give me your thoughts all for or opposed.

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Research proves they're unreliable and they're not objective at all once you get higher than 3 where the strength becomes subjective based on the rater and proper stabilization and positioning. 
I think they can be helpful for low level patients but functional measures like STS and TUG are much more objective, repeatable, reliable and obviously functional.

> **Response 2:** Thereâ€™s no harm in doing it. If you donâ€™t want to use it for goal setting then fine, but it takes 2 seconds to perform and the patient also gets feedback about how their body is performing. Our profession is ridiculous when it comes to this stuff. If you donâ€™t want to use it then donâ€™t, but donâ€™t go telling other not to do it because it does provide beneficial information when used in the grand scheme of an evaluation. I wouldnâ€™t stop using them Janell Klosterman to influence your exam findings, but may choose to use other measures for goal setting based on insurance practices

### Discussion 37: [HIGH ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2025-05-30 07:15:00 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 2 reactions, 47 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1753038892134002/

**Original Post:**
How do you all figure out times per exercise ? If I have 60 minutes with a patient and 3 -5 minutes goes towards getting subjective and objective info that leaves 55 minutes. Rest/ transition time not billable. How do you document and justify your units. Does anyone have a system they use to meet these time requirements.  Guessing how long it takes and actually timing give different results depending on the pace of the patient and I find this time consuming to get accurate. I find myself adding lots of justification to my documentation to cya and Iâ€™m a seasoned therapist.  I find this requirement very challenging. But I do it for every patient.  Even cash patients that wonâ€™t be audited because I care about being consistent.  There has to be a better way than I am using.  One patient might take 3 minutes to do 3 sets of 10 each leg and another might take 6-7 minutes. ðŸ¤·â€â™‚ï¸. Would love to hear from you

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** For the record, I 100% think you are overdoing this.  I have never documented time per exercise.  I do document time per cpt code but never per exercise or rest break or any of that.  If you are spending one on one time with your client for an hour, all of that time is billable.  My note would look something like this:

97110 Therapeutic exercise (25 minutes) - strength and flexibility training to address deficits in XYZ strength and XYZ ROM

97530 Therapeutic Activity (20 minutes) - whole body activities to retrain balance, body mechanics, posture, and movement patterns during functional activities

97140 manual therapy (15 minutes) - MFR to XYZ and MET to XYZ to address pain and dysfunction 

And then below this description of my CPT codes I would list each exercise and/or activity performed. 

Therapeutic exercises and activities performed during this session: 

1.
2.
3.
Etc

This list would be thorough with reps etc but I would not list time per. 

Then in my assessment section I will document what cues were needed and response to exercise or improvements noted and challenges still present etc. 

I think documentation is already the worst part of the job and it sounds like you are making it a hundred times worse by forcing yourself to time these things. 

If I absolutely felt I had to place a time value on each exercise because insurance required it (Medicare does not and I donâ€™t take other insurance so I havenâ€™t seen that), I guess I would simply take my 25 minutes and arbitrarily break it up next to my list of exercises until it added up.  I wouldnâ€™t even think about it.  Iâ€™d just say - letâ€™s see there are 12 exercises here - 2 min each exercise and 3 min for one - done.  I would include â€œrest time/transition timeâ€ with the exercise - we are human not robots.   Pretty sure BCBS isnâ€™t going to challenge your time.

> **Response 2:** Rest breaks are part of the exercise. You are not billing just for the time they are actively moving. Education is also billable. All that time is part of your treatment. If you're with them and providing care you should be billing.

### Discussion 39: [HIGH ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2025-03-16 04:07:21 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 1 reactions, 47 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1690636748374217/

**Original Post:**
Ortho PTâ€™s:  any advice on retraining VMO after months of atrophy and MPFL 5 weeks ago?  I have provided an NMES unit for daily use/HEP per protocol but minimal progress.

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** I donâ€™t currently work in private practice but I have worked in D1 training rooms for quite some time, so this is actually very much my domain if you donâ€™t mind me commenting here.

1) what e-stim machine are you using?AND What are the parameters you use using on the estim and what is the machine? Most portable machines (especially ones that can be purchased on Amazon) arenâ€™t actually powerful enough to provide the kind of feedback to bounce back from true AMI after surgery unless meeting certain standards. Delaware produced a study not to long back looking at units and their ability to produce true motor recruitment. I have since switched to the Chattanooga portable unit (itâ€™s about $400) and can be programmed for your patient and is a wonder!) I can forward this article. If you are using a non-portable Chattanooga device then try not using Russian estim setting, and instead trying utilizing symmetrical biphasic wavelength with 420+ phase duration and frequency of 55+ with cycle time of 10/50 for 15 mins. Still dial it up to reach borderline intolerable tetany. 

2) what are the size pads you are using? Wisconsin and a few other power 5s are utilizing 4x 8 inch pads and I got this tidbit about a year and half agoâ€” Iâ€™ll never go back. For my smaller patients Iâ€™ll still never do smaller than 2x 4 inch pads. (Also noted in the Delaware study) the size of the pad allows you to recruit larger portions of all the muscles of the quadriceps and disperses the stim over a greater area allowing you to crank up the machine even higher and provide more electrical feedback. 

3) there is some recent research published as well on the utilization of ice specifically over the knee joint itself (basically just the patella and patellar and quad tendons and you can even put the same on the backside of knee joint) DO NOT GET ICE ON ANY SURROUNDING MUSCLE TISSUE (aka not on calves or quads) for about 10 mins immediately prior to activity. It shocks the joint proprioceptors to pay better attention to the joint motion and will decrease pain response which may be contributing to the AMI itself. 

I would try all of the above changes before ditching estim â€¦ anddd I would do all of this with quad sets so itâ€™s not a difficult exercise when they are getting used to increased biofeedback/stimulation.(Estim should not be ditched before 80% LSI is achievedâ€” which is much later in most rehabs than most people discontinue itâ€” this is what OSU protocol indicates- there may even be an article I can find to support this)

Thenâ€¦

4) TKE/SLR etc can all be cheated with muscles that work when you have AMIâ€” I would switch to exercises that isolate (as much as possible the VMO) like SAQ AND ISO LAQ- although formerly believed to be taboo itâ€™s extremely important to integrate E-stim with SAQ AND utilize E-stim with iso LAQ (belted at 60-90 degâ€” due to early precautions then progress) as quickly as possible in rehab (I have integrated it as early as 2 weeks post op given my patient is comfortable with 90deg flexiÃ³n and min/no swelling)â€” estim with these two exercises specifically will help much more with the VMO (albeit the patient usually feels a bit uneasy about themâ€” especially if itâ€™s a quad tendon repair because they feel a bit of a tug)

your patient is 5 weeks post op so they should be able to tolerate both of these after changing parameters and pads with quad sets. The other commenters are right though MPFLs are much slower than an ACLR but you are right to be concerned. VMO activation should be back by week 6 at the very latest for probably good outcomes (not that it canâ€™t happenâ€¦ but itâ€™s a longer bumpier road if not)

Happy to post the articles I referenced here (most of which are extrapolations from ACLR research) and I hope this helps!

> **Response 2:** To those of you who suggested BFR, what brand of device do you use and what training did you do?  (Iâ€™m mobile)

**Date:** 2024-06-19 11:48:59 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 1 reactions, 47 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1493735568064337/

**Original Post:**

### Discussion 45: [HIGH ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2024-06-17 10:56:57 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 5 reactions, 41 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1492410138196880/

**Original Post:**
Hey guyys, i've seen some people talk about this, but honestly I don't know how I feel about it. I have a PT I work with that's COMT certified and his treatment methods are very different to McKenzie methods. Ive seen  posts where is has  been mostly COMT PT's bashing MDT PT's but honestly I'm trying to see where they find Maitland methods to be better.
A lot of the good McKenzie therapists, does not see this as "everyone does extension, they get better." They see it as a diagnostic tool. There has been several patients in my career that have been not extension responders but have been even
flexion or rotational movements. We over flex as a society given that functional activities are in some form of flexion mostly. That's why I think most people respond best to extension because we over flex which cause changes in the spine/discs.
For those who have had both Mckenzie training and Maitland training what do you feel is a more ideal method to diagnose and treat patients?
Also, do you find one method or the other to be better for different pathologies?

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** No one should be bashing any one style. The reality is, they all help for the right patients. Some patients will respond better to Maitland, others to Mckenzie, others to [insert other style]. Your job as a PT is to have as many tools in the toolbox as possible so that when you come across a patient, you can not only fit them into the right style but the right technique in that style. Not everyone is a Mckenzie patient. And that's ok. I do a lot of counterstrain- and not everyone is a counterstrain patient, and that's ok.

> **Response 2:** Iâ€™m Maitland certified and I think itâ€™s appalling that any PT is bashing another technique.  
I think the best PTs are the ones with lots of varying tools available to them.
At its core, Maitland is an assessment approach (not a treatment technique).  The accessory mobility assessment does often lead to treatment (ie. Mobilizations) but in actuality I find the treatment often aligns with preferential directional treatment (which I believe falls under the McKenzie theories).

Iâ€™m not McKenzie trained, but I have worked with McKenzie therapistsâ€¦ and I think the two approaches actually blend quite well together.

### Discussion 50: [HIGH ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2024-01-04 14:26:50 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 1 reactions, 41 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1386074998830395/

**Original Post:**
Anyone out there with experience with a tibiofibular cyst?  This is a 16 yo

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Multiple structures are often injured with a lateral meniscus injury and when repaired the meniscus is repaired but the other elements of the arcuate complex are rarely addressed.  This fixates abnormal
Mobility of the meniscus but neglects the other structures that MRI doesnâ€™t pick up.  

In my office Iâ€™d check multiple angles of varus laxity (involved vs. uninvolved), tib fib motion as well as ankle mobility (tib fib mobility is exaggerated with ankle lateral instability) and appreciate under US the other stabilizing elements of the lateral complex bilaterally.

If you have a shockwave system it can help scar the capsules and components of the lax ligaments. Electrical dry needle placement on affected ligaments can help decrease laxity, but youâ€™d have to place it on both ends of the affected tissue with the anode/cathode, and the needle placement needs to be precise.

> **Response 2:** Mri and X-ray negative when not swollen..6â€™6â€. 180 lb..basketball player
No known trauma
History of lateral meniscus surgery one year ago..this was a symptom before surgeryâ€¦no change after surgery 
HSS ortho has no diagnosisâ€¦just sent the picture to him

### Discussion 51: [HIGH ENGAGEMENT]

Is there something Iâ€™m missing with upper trap techniques for dry needling specifically?

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Describe your treatment approach - not enough information for anyone to accurately answer. 

I first learned dry needling from a more aggressive, piston-until-it-stops-twitching, treatment philosophy. I was so excited to use it in clinic the following Monday, and on the first patient I could - a pretty irritable glute tendinopathy. They did come back for a second session, thankfully, but asked that I never dry needle them again!

What Iâ€™ve found over the years is that certain body parts are more sensitive to it, that certain people are more sensitive to it, that I had eventually gravitated to â€œpiston less, stim moreâ€ before Paul Killoren coined it as a phrase, and that we always need to appreciate the symptom irritability of the patient before us.

I would treat that â€œfirst patientâ€ so much differently now than I had that day.

> **Response 2:** Soreness after needling for 24-48 hours isnâ€™t uncommon but it shouldnâ€™t be excessive, otherwise itâ€™s an indicator that your techniques were likely too aggressive. 

The upper traps have an emergent point and motor point in the same ballpark so they tend to get extra â€˜twitchyâ€™ - those local twitch responses from pistoning can definitely make someone pretty sore and achey, so have to feel out your clientele to judge whether or not you think they can handle it (and if itâ€™s even appropriate)

There's substantial evidence supporting OT-led home modifications in improving patient outcomes. Collaborating with an OT could enhance the support you're providing and ensure your patient receives the most effective care.

The patients I treat are homebound, so going to an outpatient clinic usually isnâ€™t an option. So here is my idea. To have a business that offers only wellness visits to seniors. No skilled physical therapy service, which means it is not billed to insurance and does not require a plan of care. It would be private pay only with visits as often as the client wants. Almost like having a personal trainer come to the home but with the knowledge and training of a physical therapist. I also make them sign a consent form stating that they are receiving Personal Training and not Physical THERAPY in order to cover myself. **Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Iâ€™m five years into my cash based practice in TN, and despite my best efforts, I cannot find a clear answer to this question. Thereâ€™s so much gray. Clearly, a post-op pt is a PT client. But, someone whoâ€™s having shld/back/knee stiffness, etc could fall into both the PT category and the wellness category. One thing I was well advised about is pricing. I suggest charging the same rate for PT as for Wellnessâ€¦BECAUSE your long term wellness clients will likely need PT at some point and itâ€™s too confusing (for both the patients and the business) to charge different rates for the same patient. Iâ€™m interested to see other comments here.

> **Response 2:** Cash based OT with mostly manual therapy skills/ortho/sports etc. 

How I approach wellness: 
Anything that needs more than AROM, MMT or orthopedic assessments for the evaluation I would consider skilled, for me. 
If I can do a rapid assessments and determine the cause of the problem through the three things mentioned above then I donâ€™t consider it skilled, as a LMT could also do those things. 

How I approach skilled: 
usually this is a lot easier. People will come directly from the Dr or surgeon with a plan already in place. A diagnosis, recommendations, etc. Typically the more in depth standardized evals that will take an extended amount of time to perform and interpret are my signs that it is surely skilled. 

How I avoid the grey:
I just have everyone get a Drs referral. This helps the grey area and they can also deduct from their taxes as a business expense. So itâ€™s a win win.

Is it â€œI canâ€™t get people to say Yesâ€ - outcome focused

Orâ€¦

### Discussion 86: [HIGH ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2025-10-07 18:06:42 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 5 reactions, 26 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1863401861097704/

**Original Post:**
Does or has anyone seen a patient 2x a week for maintenance? Letâ€™s say neuro conditions a like MS or Parkinsonâ€™s

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Yes but you need to have strong documentation for objective measures and impact on quality of life. You also need to vary your frequency to show the patient declines in quality of life and function with less therapy.

> **Response 2:** I have done this with 2 Medicare patients in the past. It took a lot of documentation and coordination with their provider to sign off on it when needed. The one was younger and was on disability his whole life due to a rare neuro disorder. We had years of history of him doing regular PT, discharging, declining and returning to PT. He did so well with the maintenance program! He was able to start working and eventually took a full time job that was flexible with him and he was able to get off Medicare disability! Last I heard he was still working and loving it. I was so proud of him.

### Discussion 88: [HIGH ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2024-12-13 10:33:31 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 6 reactions, 25 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1620639388707287/

**Original Post:**
Hey all. I have a severe thoracic outlet case who is awaiting thoracic surgeon appointment. Long hx of pain and has been in and out of PT for 2 years with some relief but nothing long lasting. It appears to be true vascular compression. I am trying to alleviate some of her symptoms leading up to the appointment. Are there any unique go to techniques you all have found helpful in these cases? TIA!

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Muscle energy technique and even SCS have powerful techniques for  TOS..

> **Response 2:** Check for hypermobility at the shoulder as well. My best friend from PT school had an unrelenting case of thoracic outlet and also scores a 7-8 on the Beighton scale (I forget her exact score). Whenever she would carry things, the traction on her shoulder was contributing significantly to her TOS. She was able to largely resolve her symptoms with a lot of work on shoulder stabilization. She also did a lot of work to improve her awareness so she could rely more on active vs passive support of the shoulder, especially with lifting/carrying.

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Had severe neck pain in my 20â€™s/30â€™ while working all day in clinic and doing paperwork at countersâ€¦no more issues at 47 since  beginning McKenzie exercises (progress to chin tuck with ext),  daily foam roll routine for thoracic mobility, and pec MINOR stretching.  Must gain extension and mobility at CT junction, and start out working on this every 2 hours (donâ€™t just wait til you get home at the end of the day).  Hope you feel better!

> **Response 2:** When I was first in Pilates training (before PT school), the teacher trainer encouraged us to â€œlook with your eyes, not your headâ€ because we could develop neck pain fully looking down on mats and reformers all day long. That stuck with me, along with the expectation that I should have proper posture if I was to preach it all day. Lying on a foam roller before bed also does wonders.

> **Response 1:** I'm a PT/ATC. I would check your practice act. Make sure you're comfortable with acute injury management: spineboarding, splinting,  concussion, return to play decisions. > **Response 2:** Iâ€™m a PT/ATC - I would check your state practice act for scope of practice. I would also make VERY sure that your insurance covers you. Hourly is going to be your best ask as far as pay. Games can last a really long time and coaches/schools will take advantage if youâ€™re salary. I will also say - Iâ€™ve worked as a PT and Iâ€™ve worked as an ATC. I cannot stress enough how if you are on sidelines as medical personnel you need to be ready for anything and everything as far as emergency management and be prepared for the stress of coaches and parents screaming at you from the sidelines as you make decisions. The only part of my education that was the same was eval/rehab. Sideline coverage/emergency management was a big part of my AT education and not at all part of my PT education. Sideline coverage is a whoooooole other thing (and I hate it and donâ€™t do it anymore lol).

**Original Post:**
For people who have small spaces but believe in heavy lifting, what do you feel is the most versatile piece of equipment for loading patients?

I currently have only a small room and have a pair of 15# and 35# dumbbells which has been limiting when I want to really load up for instance a deadlift. I donâ€™t have room for a barbell or plates so I was thinking about getting a heavy kettlebell but then thought maybe I should just get heavier dumbbells or something else altogether.

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Adjustable dumbbells/kettlebells (+lifting straps if grip is an issue) is one immediate change you can make. Another option is to think about what you'd like to accomplish? tissue tolerance? hypertrophy? you can use tissue tolerance exercises (e.g. drop sets, isodynamic sets, high reps) or strength endurance exercises like KB swings/snatches/squats/presses for time.

### Discussion 134: [HIGH ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2024-01-28 17:23:58 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 1 reactions, 20 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1401776953926866/

**Original Post:**
This company near me offers Pelvic Floor Therapy. They are Naturopathic Doctors, all fine, but I noticed this on their website - are they able to bill this under PT??? That doesn't seem right.

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** They can see the patient for pelvic therapyas a cash patient.  but it would be fraud to bill under Physical therapy and use the patients insurance benefits under PT if they are not a licensed PT

> **Response 2:** I do not know the answer to your question. Iâ€™m learning this too. But I do know that we as a physical therapy profession do not â€œownâ€ CPT codes, they are universally used in the medical field and selected by a provider and should only be utilized within a scope of practice. So therapeutic exercise can be used for different professions than just physical therapist. So physicians can bill 97110 and they would put their modifier instead of ours(GP). Also, the Herman and Wallace Institute allows a wide array of providers to take their courses, including Naturopathic Doctors. Many other pelvic floor courses can be taken by others. I know a personal trainer that calls her self a pelvic floor therapy specialist and states she has multiple pelvic floor certifications. So if it is the scope of treatment for Natropathic Doctors to perform and they have the skill set then I suspect they could bill. I see many different professions calling themselves pelvic floor specialists from holistic doctors to nurse midwives to personal trainers. Also, they are typically skillful with the wording calling it pelvic floor therapy instead of pelvic floor physical therapy.

Earlier this year, there were several discussions here at Uncaged about what the APTA does or does not do for our profession. Listening to the voices of protest, concern, disdain, etc., made me want to create change.   

So when APTA Private Practice asked me to run for their Nominating Committee, I couldn't stop thinking about Uncaged and the mini-revolution inspired by this movement.   

I land on the side that the APTA is helpful, because it really has helped me:  
1) Make it affordable for PT's to serve those most in need.  
2) Saved my business thousands of dollars. 
3) Saved my heart by connecting me with amazing mentors. 
4) Gave me the confidence and a venue for speaking out about what is right and what is not in our profession.  

The APTA needs your voices. So if you are a part of Private Practice APTA, please consider voting for me. 
I'll work to magnify the voice of Uncaged Clinicians in shaping the future of small business in Physical Therapy.

> **Response 1:** Thatâ€™s an â€œa la carteâ€ service. If cupping is the only thing they want, you can just charge cash for a wellness/recovery visit. PT is a full eval and YOU decide whether cupping is the most effective tool to use. I do PT and also provide DN and recovery massage as a la carte servicesâ€¦.although i will mention that some PTs prefer not to do this bc what ends up happening is that patients JUST come for those services and donâ€™t actually make the huge improvements like they could. Right now, providing a la carte services align with my business goals but i can definitely see people prefer not to provide it. Make sure youâ€™re charging for your time appropriately!! Massage therapists make at LEAST $1/minute and thatâ€™s excluding tip, mobile services, and additional modalities. My rates are $2.5-3/minute. I know you didnâ€™t askâ€¦.but I figured Iâ€™d just put it out there. Hope that helps!!  ðŸ˜Š

> **Response 2:** If youâ€™re wanting to submit for reimbursement it needs to be a physical therapy visit, meaning you would need to follow your practice act, which I would assume means evaluation. I perform evaluations and treat the same day every time. 

If youâ€™re not performing â€œphysical therapyâ€ and are doing â€œwellnessâ€ then you would not use CPT codes and not submit any sort of super bill.

> **Response 1:** Dry needling is included in my plan of care and I never do it without exercise or neuro re-ed to follow up. They are the most versatile needles Iâ€™ve found for all the ways I utilize it in my practice

> **Response 2:** PT is not a drive-through or ala carte service. Dry needling should be part of an overall plan of care.

### Discussion 167: [HIGH ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2024-12-17 19:58:50 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 4 reactions, 16 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1623888151715744/

**Original Post:**
For PTâ€™s who do diagnostic ultrasound. Do you charge separately for that? Do you offer those services to local sports teams if they need a quick ultrasound post injury to potentially diagnose something?

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** I charge as an adjunct to an initial evaluation. Do your ortho or rheum style exam to get a hypothesis list and use the most sensitive tests you can to whittle that list down. Once your list is more appropriate after the objective exam, add in the US for a specific exam with a write up of findings. 

If insurance that you are contracted with doesnâ€™t cover it then charge separately. If you have your RMSK you should not be charging under $100 for that service.

If I had time I would offer to local sports teams, but all of my kids play sports and Iâ€™m always on the sideline with one of my portable units in case of an injury and they know it. Itâ€™s a helpful screening tool to determine necessity for PT and referrals.

> **Response 2:** Check your state practice act to confirm scope of practice.

### Discussion 168: [HIGH ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2024-08-14 18:39:27 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties

**Original Post:**

**Community Responses:**

### Discussion 170: [HIGH ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2023-08-19 14:36:03 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties

**Original Post:**
Dry needling experts....

What is the research/your clinical experience on dry needling and knee pain in runners? Assuming that all the other bases are being covered as far as mobility, strengthening, running technique etc, is there value to doing dry needling in this population?

I'm editing this for clarification. I'm NOT interested in doing this on a patient...I AM the patient in this case.  I am training for the New York City marathon and I've been having some knee issues lately.  So I wanted to learn more about the effectiveness of it for myself and I would go to someone who does it if it is worth my time.

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** It depends on where the pain is.  Iâ€™ve done guided needling for a while and you can pretty much cure symptoms and pathology if done correctly.  Working on producing the research to support this.  To answer the question, patellar issues, quadriceps tendon issues, capsular issues around the patellofemoral joint - all very amenable to dry needling. Can stiffen up partially torn ligaments as well (superficial ones).

Try it,  you should find some benefits.

> **Response 2:** I have found it very helpful, especially some areas that are hard to get to manually. Recently had someone with strained post tib and ran half marathon with no pain after one session of dry needling with estim. Obviously results arenâ€™t always that quick, but I would also argue it would be harder for me to address manually and his race was 5 days after treatment. 
But also depends on state as NY doesnâ€™t allow PTs to dry needle.

### Discussion 174: [HIGH ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2023-05-02 15:38:04 | **Topic:** 14-continuing-education-ceus
**Engagement:** 4 reactions, 16 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1233503754087521/

**Original Post:**
Hello all! What do you recommend as a clinical certification to make you stand out when opening a practice? Iâ€™m an OT and have about 10 years of experience but I feel like home health has made me so bogged down by assessment visits Iâ€™m having a hard time getting back to the reason I became an OT which is the treatments and seeing patients progress! I have a background in neuro but I would love to branch out to strength training and fall prevention /longevity area. Any guidance would be appreciated!

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** I wouldn't waste time or money on a clinical certification as your patients/clients/referral sources don't care about that. 

Invest in learning about: marketing, sales, automation.

> **Response 2:** I took the Modern Management of Older Adults 3-part certification series offered through the Institute of Clinical Excellence (PT on ICE).  It was a fantastic series that I really loved and would highly recommend.  OTs can take it as well.

I'm not sure how much a clinical certification actually means to the average potential client; however, I am planning to pursue the GCS for PTs because I want the knowledge.  I don't know if it will help to have those initials behind my name, but it won't hurt.  

So I think it really depends on what you want for yourself.  If you want a business where you are not really involved in patient care, then a certification probably won't make a difference.  If you want the knowledge, then do it for that reason.

> **Response 2:** May be hard to get good strength testing because of stroke but how is that L5 myotome? There are times where symptoms match with MRI and give a better idea of the problem.

SLP question:
When a doctor says they have a potential referral for you but first wants to know if you are certified in Parkinsonâ€™s for swallowing and speechâ€¦

What do you say to them to explain? 
How do I say this without sounding cocky?

Even though I have not taken the LSVT LOUD courses, I am fully competent to use evidence based practice voice treatment to increase vocal intensity, interpret findings from a videofluoroscopic swallow study, train the patient on safe swallow compensatory strategies and/or diet recommendations. I also am competent in using evidence based practice techniques using expiratory muscle strength training to improve voice and airway protection at the same time to reduce risk of hospitalization from aspiration pneumonia, which is the number one cause of mortality in those with PD.

### Discussion 236: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2023-02-03 15:36:26 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 0 reactions, 14 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1174033243367906/

**Original Post:**
Admins, please delete this if it's inappropriate. 
For those of you experienced in treating Parkinson's, do you often see exacerbation of disease post surgery...ie joint replacement?

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Donâ€™t quote me on this but what is it the average person takes 3 days of exercise to recover per each day in a hospital 
With PD itâ€™s like 7-10 

I treat tons of PD patients like 70% of our practice and anecdotally - everything gets worse after surgery and typically double to triple what a non PD time frame is to get better  post surgery.  

Side note I have seen some amazing improvements with patients post DBS  immediately out of surgery. But then again that was specifically for their PD symptoms.

> **Response 2:** I experienced this a lot in inpatient rehab! We'd get total knees with Parkinsons there more for PD than knee. Def a flare up after or also felt/wondered if their medicine wasn't as effective right after surgery. Would usually phrase it as "your body requires extra time to process, including your healing." Completed less TherEx and a lot more Big movement work outs. 
The pain associated with improving range would also trigger more sx. As long as the range was functional we limited heavy interventions to improve to full(at least in acute phase). Same with new learning for compensatory strategies depending on where they were in their disease progress. Focused on what they felt was most important: usually going to the bathroom, but could care less about shoes kind of compromise in independence goals.

**Original Post:**
Hi. Has anyone done a service exchange and seen a client for free? I'm an OT who is also pelvic floor therapist and I'm 31 weeks pregnant. I met a doula who would like me to see her for pelvic floor therapy in exchange for her doula services for me. Do you just modify or add in your paperwork to state something like - this contract guarantees 1 OT pelvic floor evaluation and 9 treatment sessions in exchange for complete douls services per ____'s contract." Or better language than that but still the same concept. Is there anything else I need to do in particular for this type of relationship and contract? Thanks for any help.

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** I am always wary of trades. Even if it seems even. Both your services have a monetary value, why not pay her and she pays you? 
And I his is 100% playing devils advocate what if she canâ€™t attend your birth for some reason and you have already provided pelvic health services? Does she pay you back? What if you have a precipitous birth and she is only there for 20 minutes? Is it fair in exchange for 10 hours of OT or if she is with you for 36 hours because you have a long labor do you owe her money? I would make all these clear in the contract if you decide to go ahead. Ps Iâ€™m a pelvic health OT too :)

> **Response 2:** I always break down the cost, and apparently Iâ€™m the odd man out- but I LOVE trades ðŸ¤£. Been doing it for years! For doula services, Iâ€™d just divide her payment by your hourly rate and see how many sessions that gets, and put that in the contract. Barter systems are freaking fantastic IMO ðŸ™Œ

So if the doula costs $1200 and you charge $200/eval and $150/session, sheâ€™d get 1 eval and 6.67 sessions, which Iâ€™d round down and give her the extra $100 back to make it an even trade.

### Discussion 255: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2025-12-28 15:42:37 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 2 reactions, 12 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1933100824127807/

**Original Post:**
Would love to hear thoughts from anyone who has done programs instead of packages for their practice. 

Iâ€™ve heard of this idea and played with in my head a bit and like the concept. I like the idea of committing to a program and outcome instead of set number of visits.

Am curious to hear from those who have done it how they have broken that down? And pros/cons or other things to consider from doing it?

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** I personally love the idea of programs as it makes your offer much more about what the patient actually cares about. I often compare it to someone remodeling my kitchen - I don't pay them to come to my house for 5 days, I pay for a remodeled kitchen! The challenge with PT obviously is that it isn't just reliant on the PT but also the patient doing what they're supposed to. 

That is where programs are very helpful -- you have a little more clarity on outcomes as your program is usually tied to a specific injury/diagnosis. I also love the idea of a "conditional guarantee" where if the patient meets certain requirements (does their HEP, etc) then you can offer some sort of guarantee on outcomes. Something that 95% of clinics aren't doing and helps you stand out from the pack. 

My wife's clinic implemented something like this and it has made a huge difference. She says it also makes selling much easier for her as she has a program for their specific injury and you're just selling that vs. trying to guess how many sessions someone will need. She priced it at what she thought the max amount of sessions would need to be for her program -- if the patient gets better sooner than that then they are happy as they reached their outcomes faster and she is happy as that boosts her profit.

> **Response 2:** Benji Toh I think it can work well for things like post op ACL, hamstring strains, plantar fasciitis to name a couple. I havenâ€™t tried this but I think it could work.

---

### Discussion 256: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2025-04-16 13:59:37 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 0 reactions, 13 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1718943592210199/

**Original Post:**
I need some ideas to increase flexion/scaption ROM after a reverse TSA. I have a patient who sustained a humerus fix during a snow skiing accident and required a subsequent reverse TSA. I suspect the pre-surgical trauma is slowing his progress  Heâ€™s 11wks S/P and still having significant painâ€¦and his flexion ROM has been around 130 degrees for the last couple weeks. Clearly, Iâ€™m doing about 20 min of PROM and mobs twice weekly. His scap mobility is good.  Itâ€™s the glenihumeral joint and lats that seem stiff. Ultimately, Iâ€™d like to see him get about 150 deg flexion.  I appreciate any ideas.

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** I'd stop trying to get more range. That's about as good as you are going to get a rTSA. He is probably in more pain because you are pushing the joint beyond what it should do. Work on mid range stability and correct muscle activation and he should continue to improve. I'd also do a lot of STM to the infra and teres muscles as well

> **Response 2:** A reverse TSA to 150 may not be a realistic goal

### Discussion 259: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2024-11-07 13:03:09 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 4 reactions, 11 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1594615154643044/

**Original Post:**
Just started my own cash based practice in Richmond, VA but still trying to figure out my niche as I find clients. I have a personal training background before becoming a PT and I work outpatient and SNF currently (both per diem). 

Iâ€™ve been thinking of taking some courses to specialize in something to set me apart. My two thoughts have been pelvic floor and TMJ. Anyone work specifically in these areas and have recommendations or input on con ed to get started?

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** My advice would be to only enter a specialty if you're passionate about it, not because you think it will get you clients. As a pelvic floor therapist, you have to be knowledgeable about pregnancy/postpartum/reproductive care, gastroenterology, urogynecology, menopause, etc. so one or two courses is not going to cut it, it's a lifetime of learning!
That being said, we were all beginners at one point so if you are truly passionate, you'll want to start with Pelvic Health Level 1 through either the APTA or Herman & Wallace. But just know that it doesn't stop there and you will need to continue to take many, many courses to truly excel as a pelvic floor therapist as there's so much about this specialty we don't learn in school.

> **Response 2:** Hi Jazlyn! Your personal training background can be a great way to set you apart! I agree with Rachel's recommendation to only enter a specialty if you're passionate about it.

**Original Post:**
Has anyone does back to back sessions with a patient before? For example: Monday and Tuesday.

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** I always envision each session an extension of the last, you either diversify or you stack on what was already done. 

However,  if you're not making the POC for your patients and doing what you believe is right for their situation you're giving away your autonomy.

> **Response 2:** Depends on the diagnosis.

Total knee, and we're working on hard stuff that I KNOW requires recovery time? No. Not doing back to back. 

Balance stuff? Tons of room to mix things up. Can easily do back to back, even if the activities are moderate intensity for that individual.

### Discussion 281: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2023-02-27 19:55:43 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 0 reactions, 12 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1192409451530285/

**Original Post:**
Quick question:
Iâ€™m PT. Do I need a special certification to perform a Functional Capacity Test? Pt is applying for disability. 
TIA

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** The words you choose to use here are important.

A functional capacity evaluation as a very specific test. I used to perform them and yes, I went through the training in with certified.

But my guess is nobody is asking for an FCE.

SS and disability doesnâ€™t require an FCE. 

https://secure.ssa.gov/apps10/poms/images/SSA4/G-SSA-4734-U8-1.pdf

> **Response 2:** It really depends on who is paying for it and what they require. Can I evaluate ones functional capacity and report it to you? Yes. Am I certified in Isernhagen? Not anymore. Does that stop case managers from requesting that I evaluate a client's ability to safely climb a step stool and retrieve a 50# item from overhead as part of a progress note or reevaluation? No it does not. Do I offer 8 hour FCEs anymore? Nope.

### Discussion 286: [HIGH ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2023-05-06 10:54:37 | **Topic:** 14-continuing-education-ceus
**Engagement:** 10 reactions, 7 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1236086893829207/

**Original Post:**
Hi everyone!
I am collaborating with a family-friendly gym to provide a workshop and then an 8 week class focused on pelvic floor and returning to the gym postpartum. The goal being that at the end of the 8 weeks, participants feel comfortable joining the regular group classes without discomfort, leaking, or doubting that they can keep up because they will know how to modify movements. The workshop will be one hour and used to draw interest to get people to sign up for the class. 
I would love your thoughts and ideas on what should be included in either the workshop or the class itself. This is my first time partnering in the community like this!
Thank you so much in advance.

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Try to offer it at a time of day where moms are most likely to be able to find coverage for the baby (e.g., evenings) - this was a huge barrier for me postpartum if classes were mid day

> **Response 2:** Fellow PFPT here. I think in the workshop Iâ€™d hit the whatâ€™s common vs normal piece first (leaking, pain, hyper mobility, etc). I just took a class where we talked about teaching people how to palpate sit bones and pubic and tail bone to help visualize and feel for a pelvic floor contraction. I like that since you obviously canâ€™t do a real PF exam in a group setting. 
Iâ€™d focus on teaching some general recruitment, coordination, and relaxation activities focusing on breath, TA, and PF (canister). Then you can build on that with functional movement in your actual class. 
This sounds like such a fun opportunity for you!

### Discussion 291: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2024-11-14 12:00:35 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 3 reactions, 10 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1599898947447998/

**Original Post:**
Does it state anywhere in Medicare guidelines a specific frequency for maintenance therapy? Have a couple patients with Parkinsonâ€™s who have plateaued but would like to cont to see them 2 times per week

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** I just document as much as I can and reduce frequency gradually. As frequency changes I make sure to repeatedly test functional measures to assess for a decline in function and other risks like hospitalization, pressure sores, potential for increased level of care needs, and falls based on gait speed and other tests (4 square step test, DGI, TUG, ect). Also documenting caregiver training and feedback from caregivers on what theyâ€™re observing in function and concerns.

### Discussion 299: [HIGH ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2023-03-21 17:58:16 | **Topic:** 14-continuing-education-ceus
**Engagement:** 15 reactions, 4 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1207106743393889/

**Original Post:**
Here is an entrepreneurial story that I believe all of us can learn from:

There once were two bakeryâ€™s next to each other in my town. Nothing Bundt Cakes and Swirl Bakery. They sit less than 50 feet away from each other.

If any of you know Nothing Bundt Cakes, you know what you are getting when you walk in: Delicious cakes, ready to go, and a great experience. Everyone who works there is always so kind and ready to serve.  Ever notice how they even come around from the counter to greet you when you walk in?

Then there is Swirl Bakery: a fantastic bakery. They had cakes, cookies, cupcakes, scones, breadsâ€¦everything you could possibly imagine. Everything was really good there. It was just an okay experience when you walk in, depending on who was working. 

So, what can we get from this?

Two things:

1) The power that comes from being EXCEPTIONAL at one thing. What is that for you and your company?

2) The difference it truly makes when you are greeted with a smile EVERY TIME you come in.

Now, I donâ€™t know why they actually closed down. But, they were in our town for more than 10 years and I would be led to believe as a customer than 1 (or both) of the above reasons led to them closing up shop.

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** We have Nothing bundt cakes here in Downingtown.  Love the cakes.

### Discussion 304: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2024-12-15 19:42:05 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 0 reactions, 11 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1622388588532367/

**Original Post:**
For those who treat chronic pain:

What does your Perfect Patient Plan look like?

I feel I will need to modify compared to my regular MSK patients.

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** It involves a lot of pain neuroscience education

> **Response 2:** Not sure about a â€œperfect patient planâ€ as Iâ€™m not familiar with that phrase. 

I use trauma neuroscience as a method to address the root cause of the symptoms. I focus on increasing specificity of interoceptive awareness of the specific tissues involved. I also help the patient create internal safety/stability so the brain can finally process the innate memory that contributed to the altered interoceptive awareness and limited processing from involved tissues the first place. 

Ideally patient will experience significant changes after each visit while simultaneously increasing their sense of internal safety and stability. This leads to a larger window of tolerance, resolution of pain, and significantly less recurrence of symptoms.

### Discussion 310: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2023-03-19 16:23:16 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 0 reactions, 11 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1205758546862042/

**Original Post:**
Are we as PT's able to utilize something like ethyl chloride or any other numbing spray prior to Dry Needling? If so, what do you use?
I have had a few patients ask me if I had any sort of numbing spray.

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Not sure if we are allowed to use it or not or not, but a big part of the mechanism behind dry needling is the gate control theory of pain. Without the painful stimulus from the needle, you lose that potential neuropathic mechanism of healing. Still get the increased blood flow from micro trauma and twitch response but I think thatâ€™s an important piece of the healing process.

> **Response 2:** I don't think a numbing spray would be of much help as the initial price of the skin is minimal in my experience.   It wouldn't help with the deep ache people experience or stronger twitches.   As some said earlier,  there are alternative treatment options.   I frequently use SCS for patients who are needle phobic or just don't want dry needling.   I personally don't find it quite as effective but try to accommodate people as much as much as possible

---

### Discussion 323: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2025-11-25 13:18:09 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 1 reactions, 10 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1907357323368824/

**Original Post:**
Asking for a friend (that friend is me)- 

I bought my 17 year old volleyball playing son a massage gun for Christmas because "all the boys use them". He is interested in pursing a career in PT/athletic training/etc. I want him to use it correctly and show his teammates how to use the device correctly. I went down a YouTube rabbit hole looking for reputable videos on the science/physiology/technique but couldn't determine which videos were really legit/evidence based. Are the TheraBody videos any good? 

I thought this group would love able to help me out. Please post links of videos, articles, webinars, etc on massage guns that would be age/audience appropriate. Thanks in advance

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Personally I do a lot of hands on manual therapy and I hate the massage guns for rehab. I think they're not bad in muscle recovery after a workout, but find them to be of limited use in real rehab techniques. The oscillation is really only good when used directly on the muscle, but I find most problems in the tendons and bursae around the joint, kinda hard to get into those areas with a massage gun, the head attachments are usually too bulky, and you cant really "feel" the tissue for inflammation. I'd rather just use gua sha tools or my hands.

> **Response 2:** Honestly I donâ€™t think there is really a â€œcorrectâ€ way. Itâ€™s great for recovery if youâ€™re sore but otherwise itâ€™s mostly just a feel good. I treat a ton of runners and triathletes and mostly tell them to just avoid overdoing it to the point of bruising themselves- something I have seen way too often!

### Discussion 337: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2024-12-25 20:39:53 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 0 reactions, 10 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1629961041108455/

**Original Post:**
Merry Christmas to those who celebrate!

If cost was not a factor, what is the absolute best knee brace for an adult in their 30s who likely has Ehlers-Danlos?

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** I treat almost exclusively eds. I usually start with a compression sleeve. I find that braces can sometimes over correct and they're so mobile that a slight over can actually just push them the other way.  If that's not enough I've had good luck with the ACL braces (like don joy)

> **Response 2:** Futuro are my favorite. Target sells them. She can even try them on at target too before she buys. I give them to my professional athletes. They are sleek/not bulky yet supportive. I like the neoprene Velcro adjustable brace. They have a few different options for knee bracing but I like the discrete ones. The sleeves, I recommend those more for compression or lymphedema needs but people love the copper compression sleeves. I also specialize with hEDS in professional athletes and EDS in my private practice. I usually like something that maintains mobility but has great compressive support to reduce pain and discomfort.

---

### Discussion 354: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2022-10-15 16:08:35 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 1 reactions, 9 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1082285542542677/

**Original Post:**
Anyone doing concussion evaluations/assessments? I was curious if Physical Therapy is classified as a â€œqualified healthcare providerâ€ for the following CPT codes. 
ï¿¼Can we bill for the evaluation under code 96132 or would that be our regular PT low/moderate/high complexity evaluation?

96132/96133 (neuropsychological evaluation by physician or other qualified healthcare provider first 60 min/additional hours)

96138/93139 (psychological/neuropsychological test administration performed by physician or other qualified healthcare professional first 30 minutes/additional 30 minutes) 

Any insight is appreciated, thank you!

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Cognitive screening measures (impact, moca, etc) are not billable or equivalent to standardized cognitive assessment performed and interpreted by Neuropsych or SLP. Screening measures can be given by nurses or athletic trainers. I can't imagine these CPT codes would fall under the scope of practice of a PT

> **Response 2:** Depends on what you are doing in your evaluation and treatment. Are you using anything like imPact?

Editing to add: Iâ€™m in Texas bc I know that probably makes a difference.

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Since you have your DPT, in Texas you can technically evaluate and treat for 10 days without an order.  

Iâ€™m sure you have an established assessment/ evaluation process, and treatment would be the pre-natal massage as indicated.  The pre-natal population sees their providers regularly, so when the pre-natal client leaves your care after the first massage, they could take a referral/order for continued pre-natal massage for the referralâ€™s signature.  Then youâ€™re covered for longer than 10 days with the signed order.  

The PT Rules state a reassessment is required every 60 days at a minimum. 

Thanks for providing your much-needed services in the Permian Basin!

> **Response 2:** You would need to attend an accredited lmt school and have an lmt license in conjunction with your PT. They may let you out of some of the classes.

### Discussion 396: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT]

> **Response 1:** My opinion is that exercise is wellness if itâ€™s not addressing a specific established diagnosis, AND therex become exercise after youâ€™ve taught it. I think that for too long PT has been a crutch for people who need accountability.

> **Response 2:** As Abby Regan said, the minute the conversation becomes â€œI want you toâ€¦â€ vs â€œyou needâ€¦â€ itâ€™s wellness. How many times have patients begged you not to DC them from therapy even when itâ€™s not medically necessary. â€œBut I just canâ€™t do it with it you, you encourage me and I do it better with you.â€ 

What they are asking for is personal training paid for by insurance. Thatâ€™s a come to Jesus conversation about what MC SHOULD have to pay for vs what the PATIENT should have to pay for

### Discussion 401: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2025-04-18 12:41:41 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 6 reactions, 5 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1720510498720175/

**Original Post:**
For those of you who treat out of a gym with excellent trainers.  How do you balance the exercise you do in PT with the gym activities they already perform.  I find myself educating on how to do things, the trainers and I talk, but I rarely have them do exercise in the office because it seems like overkill.   Asking because we're looking to expand and this is a question I have moving forward.  TIA

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** I work at a clinic that sees a lot of fitness forward clients meaning they have a personal trainer, participate in boutique fitness, etc and I find these patients are typically the ones that need a lot of education on volume management and dosage. I spend a lot of time on programming for these patients, so maybe your time can be spent there. I also find these patients really benefit from a combination of manual therapy and neuro reeducation type exercises and/or exposure to different workout types like EMOM or AMRAP to vary the overall intensity of their workouts.

> **Response 2:** I usually have the patients do the PT exercises as accessory work/mobility work. If I know the main muscle groups are going to be hit in workouts i focus on the smaller muscles, motor control, mobility, specific tempos or range of motion, etc that aren't targeted in their workouts.

---

But being new practice  (I donâ€™t want initially to loose patients by serving only  a subgroup , so  Shd I also include in my website or brochures that other ortho conditions/ wellness / pain mgmt will be treated as well â€¦.. or Shd I stick to one main focus area â€¦.. ALL SUGGESTIONS/ ideas are most welcome â€¦ thank you

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Myself (PT) and an OT colleague opened a neuro focused brick and mortar clinic about a year and a half ago and there is soooo much need for neuro specific care out there, people just donâ€™t necessarily realize that not all PTs have the same expertise in treating neuro conditions. We have marketed only as neuro specialists and while it took some time and some work to get our name out there as the place to go for neuro PT and OT, it was definitely the right way to go. We have received referrals for other diagnoses and typically donâ€™t turn people away if they donâ€™t have a neuro specific diagnosis, but we donâ€™t specifically market to other populations.

> **Response 2:** Keep it narrow.

It feels scary, but it's SO MUCH EASIER for folks to recommend you to their peers when they know EXACTLY what you do and the problems you solve.

(Easier doesn't mean it's the only way. And, you very well might see an initial dip in... Everything. Be prepared, be patient, and keep stepping forward.)

### Discussion 431: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2024-05-10 22:20:27 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 3 reactions, 6 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1467792977325263/

**Original Post:**
Anyone in this amazing group have success marketing directly to total joint replacement patients?  Any good tips/ tricks with this group? My mobile med B practice is nearly entirely geriatric deconditiong/ falls (which I love) but I'd love to get more into providing rehab post TKA and THA. Currently nearly all the Orthos in my area go to corporate owned dime-a-dozen type clinics and I'd love to break into that population more. Thanks!

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Are any of them participating in the Medicare bundling?  I am mobile and I contract with an agency who works with an orthopedic surgeon who does the minimally invasive total knee replacement. It's same-day surgery patients are sent home, we see them post-op day one, teach dressing changes, monitor the incision and do the PT, all billed under outpatient benefits. 
Most patients get about 12 visits some don't need them all some need more. Way cheaper this way than using home health.

And the physician usually does a call on day one post-op and on day three. 

The company I contract for estimates that using us costs about $1200. If they start with home health it's so much more costly.  For difficult patients we do a pre-op visit so we can teach them everything they need with regards to equipment assessing the shower all of that.

> **Response 2:** Prove to the orthos why your personal attention is better, and I think they'll be swayed

---

### Discussion 444: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2025-11-03 02:24:11 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 2 reactions, 6 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1887718881999335/

**Original Post:**
Any advice on starting a mobile, cash based dry-needling service? 
Thinking of primarily marketing to ENT and Dentist in small town for TMJ and Headache cases first and then branching out based off word-of-mouth.

Also wanting to avoid conflict of interest with my current employer.

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Whatever you do, please do not market it as a dry needling practice. You are either a PT, an OT, a chiropractor, etc. Dry needling is one tool in your overall toolbox and should only be used as part of a holistic plan of care, regardless of the diagnosis or patient in front of you. We are fighting for multiple professions to be able to dry needle in multiple states and using dry needling as a standalone intervention is putting many of us at risk for losing the privilege. Thanks in advance.

> **Response 2:** I would suggest you join the formal Uncaged Clinician coaching group. Not only would it give you the exact steps that you need, but also specific ongoing support. David Bayliff is incredible.

---

### Discussion 453: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2024-09-05 22:23:14 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 0 reactions, 7 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1546769606094266/

**Original Post:**
Hi all, does anyone happen to know if with telehealth (e.g. on the Jane App), are we allowed to treat anyone in the U.S. even if we are licensed in 1 state? E.g. If I am licensed in Hawai'i, can I treat patients who move to or live in California, Washington, Utah, etc? Much appreciated ðŸ™.

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** My understanding is wellness yes but physical therapy skilled treatment based services you need to be licensed within the state you are providing those services whether teletherapy or in person. Each state has different laws and regulations

> **Response 2:** My understanding is as a licensed PT, OT, SLP you can only treat in person or via telehealth in the state you are licensed in

### Discussion 472: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2025-08-09 21:48:56 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 1 reactions, 6 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1811873819583842/

**Original Post:**
I was asked to be on the sideline for a local football team, during their games. I haven't the faintest Idea on what all I need to know or be able to do? Alongside would be an ortho surgeon who is the official "Team Doc" but he apparently sucks and isn't that great to work with. I know the head coach very well and he has been an old patient of mine who I helped avoid a surgery. He sends me a lot of his football guys. But I just want to know what all I need to know or be able to do?

I just rcently started doing a lot of ultrasound and I'm in the process of getting my RMSK. So I showed him a lot of the ultrasound that we do and also a lot of the manual muscle testing/force plate eval's we can do and of course dry needling.

My main goal was to establish communication with the athletic trainer and directly with the coaches as well in case they ever need to send me someone urgently, etc etc

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** I see this topic brought up a lot. In short - don't do it. I'm a DPT, OCS, SCS, and a host of other certs. Part of getting and maintaining the SCS involves venue coverage and an Emergency Response for Athletes cert. It takes training and preparation to be ready and qualified for venue coverage. If you haven't done the prep work, dont try to provide the venue coverage.

> **Response 2:** Even if you have an idea of what to do, if you don't have the appropriate training, do not do it. You can be held liable in the event of an unfortunate incident. Message this fact to the coach and offer your urgent orthopedic care only and not on the sideline.

### Discussion 482: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2024-04-04 18:13:08 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 1 reactions, 6 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1444274006343827/

**Original Post:**
Hello everyone! 
Apologies if this question has been asked here earlier. 

What kind of safety measures do you guys follow for yourself when you go to someone's house for home PT? I carry mace spray and have Google location on on my phone. But should I be doing anything else? 

I am a petite female and I am very strong for my size, but I'm just thinking and looking for ideas to keep my and my husband's mind at ease. 
Thanks.

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** 1- know your quick exit just in case
2- sometimes I leave my airpod in my ear( and kind of cover with my hair
3- there were times where I would pretend to be taking or make a quick  call just to tell my husband where I am even if he is not on the other line ( of course I asked to be excuse for the call)
4- ask if they have pets- I went to a pt's house with a pet snake as fat as my thighs I almost had a heart attack
   Dogs- i am not scared of dogs but my PT friend doing HH got bitten by a dog who seemed mellow in the beginning
5- I try to keep the door open if I go a pt's private room esp if the pt is kind of off
6- I don't take food or drinks of they offer or if I take it- then toss it later- unless I am sure it is 100% safe
6- I bring a lab coat in case I am not wearing  scrubs- esp in not so good neighborhood- I put it on- 
 - my friend coached me this- he said people will respect you and believe you are out there helping sick people - :-)

> **Response 2:** I treat elderly folks with neurological conditions... generally I only treat folks I can outrun, but I keep my phone on me, and read the room.  If the vibe feels a little off, I try to make sure I'm closest to the door, and am mindful of who else is in the home.  I haven't felt the need for anything else. I had one inappropriate patient who said some things, I had to address, but nothing that bad.

### Discussion 484: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2024-01-28 04:33:34 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 1 reactions, 6 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1401416203962941/

**Original Post:**
Question, if youâ€™re doing a straight cash based, al acart type side hustle - do you still do a direct access consent form? Iâ€™m in VA for context. I have no intentions on working with insurance. My side hustle is cash and performance enhancement/proactive in focus.

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** The short answer is if anyone is coming to you without a referral, regardless of the payment source, you must have a Direct Access Consent signed. 
The specifics of that form depend on your state practice act.

> **Response 2:** Cash vs insurance are billing models.  No matter which billing model you choose, direct access has to do with the service youâ€™re offering being licensed vs non-licensed. If you are treating a diagnosis or providing a treatment and that classifies as a PT licensed service in your state, you have to follow direct access laws of your state. Example in Texas - youâ€™d need to follow DA laws to administer hands on treatments and dry needling, but not consulting/coaching. Example 2 - youâ€™d need to follow DA laws to treat knee pain (there is an ICD-10 code for knee pain so itâ€™s a diagnosis), but not to help someone improve performance during squatting. Hope this helps.

---

Has anyone worked in this scenario before and what was a fair price to charge. ï¿¼

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** I would be very cautious to practice within your scope of practice. In PA, PTs have lost their license because of practicing as a ATC.  You need to be a â€œfirst responder â€œ and be certified in emergency care as a minimum.  Lots of liability issues. My company has many school contracts but we use ATCs (then PTs as needed for post injury treatment ). We also have PTs but they are ATCs too.

> **Response 2:** Thank you for the input on this! Iâ€™m in Colorado and I had told the AD that I wanted to look into the state laws as to what I can and cannot do and specified that I was not an athletic trainer but rather a PT but that were allowed to do splinting/ taping ankles, assess concussion, and assess acute injury as well as give ice for injuries etc

### Discussion 507: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2024-03-23 22:43:25 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 2 reactions, 5 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1436385733799321/

**Original Post:**
Does anyone here have any experience writing up a business proposal working with a club sport for high school students? And how did you determine your pricing?
Background: I have the opportunity to work with a volleyball club that I actually used to play for when I was in high school and have contacted the club director about providing PT/wellness services to the clubâ€™s players. Initially, I would do an injury screen to at least 1/4 of the players and give them 1-2 exercises if the screen came back positive for anything concerning. There is also the possibility of me providing 5-15 minutes of manual therapy to the players 1x/week during their regular practices. Both of these services would be paid for by the club and if any family/player wanted to hire me separately then that would be covered by the family/player.
Thank you!!

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** If you can... leave time out of it...   

For example don't say $30 for 15 min...

Base it on $300  to cover etc

Or.    $300 a day.  Or. $300 to serve 30 kids

make it about you being available, not just in doing

Make sense?!?

> **Response 2:** A point of reference: my daughter charges 50 per kid for 55 min OH hitting 2-4 kids , another defense trainer 1:1 45 min lesson $60-70. They all do conditioning training on the side 2X week at $25 session in groups of usually 5
