### Discussion 516: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2023-03-03 14:29:58 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 0 reactions, 6 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1194913561279874/

**Original Post:**
Hello!
I have a medicare patient who i have been seeing for a left ankle fracture, she has now seen a different doctor and received a second script for a different right knee injury. As a provider can I treat her for both injuries on the same day and keep her for an hour and a half and get paid on both cases? The patient wants to know if she needs to see a different provider so she can receive treatment 2 days a week for her knee and 2 days a week for her ankle. The only problem is i am currently open 3 days a week, so i would need to see her one of the days for both appointments if she stayed with me. Would medicare reimburse me for both knee and ankle cases separately? They come from two different doctors and injuries.
Thank you!

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Anthony Maritato speaks about charging for the 2nd body part, but keep it all in one case. Tony, am I correct with this?

> **Response 2:** Do a re-evaluation, in the assessment explain why that the re-evaluation was done which is to update the plan of care to include the new condition which requires it's own POC/eval. The re-eval is going to update current s,o, and a, add new goals, update old goals, add a new plan. 

On treatment days, you're going to get reimbursed on what you did and how you treated. At that point it's no different than if they had walked in with both problems at the same time. You may need to extend treatment time, but you don't need to add a treatment day unless you want to.

---

### Discussion 518: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2023-01-09 14:54:03 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 2 reactions, 5 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1155104801927417/

**Original Post:**
HELP! I'm in Ohio for reference. I am going thru the practice act, but it is so unclear to me. 

I am a certified manual therapist and that is a big part of the way I practice. I'm still a bit confused as to my place in this therapy / wellness world. I have a fully cash practice, no insurance. If I see somebody and do any sort of manual therapy corrections of the spine or pelvis or anything like that does it automatically make it physical therapy instead of wellness? If so, do I need to have a plan of care in place in their chart? For wellness, I just do it "as needed". People seem to really like the flexibility of coming when they want and are using me effectively. However, I want to be in compliance with our state practice act, and have my malpractice insurance cover me. Generally, many of my wellness patients are one-time monthly. Since that is the case, doing a progress note every 30 days does not make sense. I'm doing more "maintenance" work. However, during those sessions, I often use manual therapy ( manipulation, joint mobs, soft tissue work, dry needling, etc.) to get them aligned so that they're exercises are more effective. 
Can someone speak to these things? Can I do whatever they need if they are paying for a service, or am I operating outside our practice act? If all I need is a plan of care, I can add that easily. If I have to send to Dr, I think it's a bigger deal just because I don't see individual patients with a high frequency. I'm more of a maintenance program.

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** If fully cash, why would you need notes at all or any sort of plan of care except as a reminder for you for the next session? No electronic record, no HIPPA. Assuming your malpractice is as a PT.

> **Response 2:** I prefer to think of wellness as â€œcould a personal trainer legally do this?â€ So I would not dry needle or manipulate a patient that Iâ€™m seeing for wellness. A massage therapist, those places that stretch people, etc. - those donâ€™t require an MD Rx, providing exercise plan doesnâ€™t require an Rx. So I would look more into what your practice act specifically puts under the term personal trainer and also what it says specifically about dry needling and manipulation.

### Discussion 536: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2025-01-28 23:21:17 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 1 reactions, 5 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1654596971978195/

**Original Post:**
How did you deal with imposter syndrome at the beginning? Iâ€™m just starting my own business (have 5 years of PT experience)  and donâ€™t feel like my services are worth charging prices I see on here for treatment/eval. I worry I donâ€™t know enough/wont help my patients the same all on my own. Is this normal or am I not ready if I feel this way?

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** I started my company with less experience than you (1 year in my own business right now).  For whatever it's worth- I had similar concerns, so I did a lot of self reflection regarding what I'd treat. What conditions do you treat that you feel you can treat better than the average PT? Market your business towards that. Get a patient with a condition you don't feel confident in? Refer them elsewhere. That's what I did, helped me feel so much better being in business by myself. 

That being said, don't rest on only treating only things you like- identify your weaknesses and work on them. Take awesome continuing education courses - they are a business expense now too!

> **Response 2:** Something that helped me when I was in PT school was even if you donâ€™t know everything you know more than your patient. Sometimes the simplest things make a huge difference in their life. Also, it gets better with experience (from the business and cash side). I used to feel guilty with a $150/hr charge but now I wonâ€™t accept less than $185 and might even consider raising those within the year. You get people who want to be there and get helped by you. Youâ€™re totally ready. Just be confident in what you do not and hold yourself responsible for what you donâ€™t and keep learning. You got this!

### Discussion 543: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2023-06-23 14:45:50 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 1 reactions, 5 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1267562597348303/

**Original Post:**
Good morning everyone. Does someone have experience working with patients after peroneal tendons repair? I have patient who had repair done many years ago and his main complaint is not being able to do SL calf raise on the surgical side along with instability. I researched some articles about peroneal tendon repair which were mostly case reports written by MDs and they reported "good" or "great" outcomes with no other details. Is there any possibility that those patients will never be able to do full calf raise on the surgical side?

My patient is 40-year-old personal trainer who is very active and exercise on daily basis.

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Thank you for all the comments! So after my eval here are some important takeaways: 1. Pt did "rehab" by himself (due to various reasons). Personally I think this is where the problem stems from. He did some exercises but I believe he did not have proper and progressive progression, as well as other set of eyes to see if he is developing compensations. 2. I learned from surgical report that his ankle was fused due to instability (ATFL and CFL) as well as his peroneus brevis was used to reconstruct the longus. 3. Based on my objective eval I deduct not only severe weakness in triceps surae complex but also in flexor digitorum longus (MMT of toes 2-5th are maybe 4-/5 at best). 4. During SL and DL heel raise pt demo'es ankle supination which looks like dominance coming form tibialis anterior and posterior . Thus my plan is to do progressive strengthening of triceps surae etc. I showed pt mobo board  but I recommended doing custom modification to have digits 2-5th do the work, not 1st ray, on top of other strengthening exercises. I plan to sprinkle in exer such as toe yoga so pt is able to learn dissociation between his 5 digits ðŸ™‚ TLDR: all we need to do is progressive overloading!

> **Response 2:** Iâ€™ve seen a few of them for post-op rehab. They all did well and were able to do SL calf raises by d/c with pretty symmetrical excursion to non-operative side.

---

### Discussion 545: [MEDIUM ENGAGEMENT] 

**Date:** 2025-12-23 21:23:28 | **Topic:** 14-continuing-education-ceus
**Engagement:** 1 reactions, 5 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1929605997810623/

**Original Post:**
Favorite in service/ free education class topics?! Would be a free 30 minute or less at a local personal training facility

Soreness vs pain? 
Posture? 
Lifting mechanics?
Targeted body part info (knee/ shoulder etc)?

I have only done one years ago for gymnasts so totally different demographic and really want to get my brain jogged on truly good topics and what im allowed to discuss in a free workshop like that. Obviously cant diagnose or treat individual issues - need a general topic to educate on! Thanks!

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Unsolicited advice since these are what blew up my mobile gig. You can position yourself as the expert that can solve their pain point no matter the issue. My favorite presentation pitch is: 
Clarity: identify exactly what you are talking about 
Authority: why you
Problem: identify it 
Solution: what it will take 
The why: why you are passionate or why there is the problem
Opportunity: what they have the chance to be part of 
Next steps: how they can engage with you 
Essence: the feelings you want the to leave with 
Capstone framework. Just pitch them with confidence

> **Response 2:** I usually ask the gym owner or head coach/personal trainer for ideas.  They most likely can tell you 2-3 things their clients complain of or would be interested in.

**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1444806146290613/

**Original Post:**
How do you best help your patients with HEP compliance? Besides talking to them about the importance/how they wonâ€™t get better just seeing me 1x per week/helping them come up with a time in their day. I know it varies patient to patient. What has worked best for you?

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Ask them how much time they are willing to commit to the hep during the eval. Then give them a clear understanding of how theyâ€™ll progress based on that. And yes definitely incorporate into daily activities. Also discuss their why during the eval to improve compliance

> **Response 2:** What is their barrier to not doing? Sometimes we overcomplicate things for patients and they feel overwhelmed. Maybe identify 2-3 things most important to them and mimic that in the exercises. If they donâ€™t feel theyâ€™re valuable to meet their goals, it doesnâ€™t matter what we say or how great the exercises are. My favorite saying is the best exercise program is one that patients are willing and able to doðŸ¤·ðŸ¼â€â™€ï¸ðŸ¤£ 
There are some apps/websites that track compliance also

### Discussion 589: 

**Date:** 2023-12-05 16:05:51 | **Topic:** 14-continuing-education-ceus
**Engagement:** 2 reactions, 4 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1367615204009708/

**Original Post:**
How would someone become a provider to offer continuing education credits to participants/students for a private company?

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** If youâ€™re providing continuing education to therapists, you need to look up the requirements for each discipline on their state board website. There can be a lot of specific requirements to meet and paperwork on your background and they usually want your CV and often a sample certificate that you plan to issue to your students. Youâ€™ll also need a detailed itinerary of your course to submit. Each state and each board within a state have different requirements and fees to pay to become an approved provider for a course. Another option is to become an AOTA approved provider, which is more intensive a process, but then you wouldnâ€™t have to apply through each state to provide your course to OTs, for example.

> **Response 2:** Itâ€™s state by state dependent, and once you are initially credentialed for the one course, some states have further credentialing where all of your courses can be pre-approved (as a CEU provider) but there are generally a few more steps including all educatorâ€™s CVs and research that is used to teach etc

### Discussion 592: 

**Date:** 2023-02-21 01:59:07 | **Topic:** 14-continuing-education-ceus
**Engagement:** 2 reactions, 4 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1187313835373180/

**Original Post:**
Hey everyone! Holding my first workshop at a dance studio in a little over a month. Do you have workshop attendees and/or the business owner of the place that's hosting the workshop sign anything in advance? I plan to have a "sign in" sheet with people to leave their contact info, but not sure if there should be any consent or written agreement signed, too. 

Finally, any tips on hosting an interactive workshop without PowerPoint or use of electronics would be greatly appreciated! Haven't done public speaking in quite some timeðŸ˜…

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Emily, I've been doing workshops since 2018 on various topics. I stay clear of ppt or any other electronic tools. I make a handout for everyone with a question that they can fill in as you go along. Use an attendee as a demo to show exactly what you do. You can sometimes pick out someone who might be a 5-minute miracle, but don't count on that being your closer. The closer should be a call to action without sounding like a used car salesman. Collect names and emails to build your distribution list. I always found 12--15 a good sweet spot for attendance. Much more it will lose it's intimacy. Less than 10 make it more personable for each person. Are you charging? Are you capping attendance? Not sure what you are doing or what state you live in, but doubt you need any sort of release. Sorry for throwing all this out there. Happy to chat- DM me

---

### Discussion 628: 

**Date:** 2025-04-10 16:51:07 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 0 reactions, 4 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1713760579395167/

**Original Post:**
Question for my fellow sports orthopedic therapists. Do you have a court shoe recommendation for pickleball? She is wide width with mild midfoot pronation.

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** New Balance, Brooks, Hoka, Asics just something she is comfortable in. Usually going shoe shopping when the feet are tired is good, and wear them around for 20 mins to determine how they feel if they are as supportive, and not rubbing anywhere. Some running stores have a treadmill inside the store to try them. Some pts we get from the podiatrist will get custom orthotics, it varies on if it helps, some take about 6 weeks. Usually a wear program few hours a day until they get used to the custom inserts. They could also get an over the counter set as needed.

> **Response 2:** I don't love Hokas for court sports since there is less lateral stability because of the rocker bottom. Those really are best only for walking and running.

### Discussion 637:

**Original Post:**
I have a friend in late 50s that has L sided neck pain. Has tried both conventional PT (with another clinic) which included dry needling.  Still has significant pain with turning his head to the L. Regarding the DN and any other manual intervention, not worth much of not followed by active intervention. If all has been assessed and appropriate intervention performed, may look at pain psych. Wondering the timeline of all this as well.

> **Response 2:** How much ROM does he have to the L?â€¦I usually let that guide my treatment (upper, mid, lower cervical)â€¦mulligan mobs with movement can be very helpful bc then you can teach them to self mob= more carryover.

### Discussion 656: 

**Date:** 2022-05-17 15:24:28 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 0 reactions, 4 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/969126983858534/

**Original Post:**
Just need a little refresh. Medicare patients need a progress note 30 days or 30 days re-assessment?

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** As of 2008 a POC can be certified up to 90 days.  the 30 day requirement ended Dec 31 2007. Source CMS chapt 15 page 171.

C.  Review of Plan and Recertification Reference:  42CFR424.24(c), 1861(r), 42CFR 410.61(e). The timing of recertification changed on January 1, 2008.  Certifications signed on or after January 1, 2008, follow the rules in this section.  Certifications signed on or prior to December 31, 2007, follow the rule in effect at that time, which required recertification every 30 calendar days. Payment and coverage conditions require that the plan must be reviewed, as often as necessary but at least whenever it is certified or recertified to complete the certification requirements.  It is not required that the same physician/NPP who participated initially in recommending or planning the patientâ€™s care certify and/or recertify the plans. Recertifications that document the need for continued or modified therapy should be signed whenever the need for a significant modification of the plan becomes evident, or at least every 90 days after initiation of treatment under that plan, unless they are delayed.

> **Response 2:** Progress Notes every 10th visit..  looking for source reference.  pg 184
Progress Reports (including Discharge Notes, if applicable) when records are requested after the reports are due.  (See definitions in section 220 and descriptions in 220.3 D); 

pg 191: 
D.  Progress Report The progress report provides justification for the medical necessity of treatment. Contractors shall determine the necessity of services based on the delivery of services as directed in the plan and as documented in the treatment notes and progress report.  For Medicare payment purposes, information required in progress reports shall be written by a clinician that is, either the physician/NPP who provides or supervises the services, or by the therapist who provides the services and supervises an assistant.  It is not required that the referring or supervising physician/NPP sign the progress reports written by a PT, OT or SLP. Timing.  The minimum progress report period shall be at least once every 10 treatment days.  The day beginning the first reporting period is the first day of the episode of treatment regardless of whether the service provided on that day is an evaluation, reevaluation or treatment.  Regardless of the date on which the report is actually written (and dated), the end of the progress report period is either a date chosen by the clinician or the 10th treatment day, whichever is shorter.  The next treatment day begins the next reporting period.  The progress report period requirements are complete when both the elements of the progress report and the clinicianâ€™s active participation in treatment have been documented. For example, for a patient evaluated on Monday, October 1 and being treated five times a week, on weekdays:  On October 5, (before it is required), the clinician may choose to write a progress report for the last weekâ€™s treatment (from October 1 to October 5).  October 5 ends the reporting period and the next treatment on Monday, October 8 begins the next reporting period.  If the clinician does not choose to write a report for the next

---

**Original Post:**
Are there any PT schools that are especially good at hand therapy?

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Do you mean continuing education companies? Or actual PT degree schools? PT school doesn't generally have a focus in one area. We learn a little of everything in a well rounded all-inclusive education,  learn some more specifics during internships and then usually specialties come later with continued education.

> **Response 2:** I remember at Columbia they had a volunteer trip to South America to do hand therapy with poor communities. The students got to do a lot of hands on work. Butâ€¦ did I find the coursework on hand therapy that great? No, I am sure you can learn the same thing at another program or buy some books and learn on your own. My suggestion would be to go to the cheapest DPT program, set up your own  clinical rotation at a clinic with a PT that specializes in hand therapy and has that patient caseload. You can seek out your own volunteer and learning opportunities at any program. Then when you graduate yogi will have less debt and more cash funds to take hand therapy continuing education classes. I am sure you could also find a hand therapy PT residency, but IMO residencies with lower salaries are predatory. You are basically doing more work by studying, doing an A+ job for less money and I call bullshit.

### Discussion 665: 

**Date:** 2022-05-27 12:12:20 | **Topic:** 14-continuing-education-ceus
**Engagement:** 2 reactions, 3 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/976036403167592/

**Original Post:**
Hello everyone! I am a Physical Therapist in Florida right now and recently applied and got my LLC to start my own business, but we are moving for an unknown amount of time to Washington D.C. As of right now, I am not planning to get my physical therapy license there but would like to see people under the umbrella of wellness still. I have my STOTT Pilates certification and am a certified life coach. I did have my NASM Personal Training certificate a long time ago but let it lapse when I became a PT. How can I do this, legally? Is there anything extra I need to do or be cautious of? How do I or can I market myself when people ask? If we do end up staying longer, I would probably get my license in Virginia and join the PT COMPACT but as of now, DC is not part of the compact so, Iâ€™m curious about how I could offer wellness services in DC, but PT in Virginia, Maryland, etc? 

This is confusing, but Iâ€™ve let that get in my way too long! Thanks

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** I've offered wellness for years. Above is correct. Also any forms before working w a client should clearly state this is not PT, it's wellness. There's not much else. Get an insurance plan or if you have just add wellness/training to it, it's cheap.

> **Response 2:** My thoughts. You can advertise your degree of a DPT (no one can take that away) but call your services all wellness services. You can not say PT though bc that is what denotes a license in our PT, DPT credential. When I tried to report someone in Michigan calling themselves an athletic trainer who wasn't an athletic trainer the board said they could only discipline a clinician who was licensed. So overall if your offering wellness and not PT and not billing for it I would assume you have alot less restrictions.

> **Response 2:** In outpatient we saw them pretty early on. Usually 1-2 weeks post. Surgeon obviously takes the lead but many of times they were very receptive to our opinion.

### Discussion 679: 

**Date:** 2024-01-09 13:12:35 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 1 reactions, 3 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1389137411857487/

**Original Post:**
Weâ€™re a neuro PT/OT clinic. Weâ€™re going to do lunch and learn at a local ENT (for our vestibular therapist) and neurologist offices this month. Any suggestions of what to bring and how to structure it?

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** I used to work for a group of ENT's-- I was the director of their vestibular PT clinic.  What they want to hear is:

-Your vestibular PT knows what he/she is doing (certified in vestibular) and gets good results. 

-You get patients in quickly, especially the "emergency vertigo" cases.  Maybe even same day if they need you to.

-You have programs for treating their chronic vestibular diagnosed patients-- meniere's, vestibular migraine, etc.

-They'll also probably want to know what type of equipment you test and treat with (I.e. frenzel lenses, CATSIB platforms, etc).  You'll also want to show them anything special about your gym or space which makes it particularly "vestibular" in nature.

-And last but not necessarily specific to ENT's-- what insurances you take and how much it will cost their patients.

> **Response 2:** We always use a local Tex mex/Mexican restaurant & have them deliver to the office & set it up. They set up the taco bar & supply all the plates, napkins & cutlery. Itâ€™s an easy clean up!

### Discussion 705: 

**Date:** 2024-10-05 18:48:50 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 2 reactions, 2 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1569629570474936/

**Original Post:**
Many of us are trying to operate in a coaching or educational capacity without establishing a patient-clinician relationship.  From a legal standpoint, are there things or language you can use to protect yourself and not accidentally cross the line?

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** I am taking a page from Mike Chua's methodology. Here is what ChatGPT said:
...
Operating in a coaching or educational capacity without establishing a patient-clinician relationship is becoming increasingly common, but it does require clear boundaries to avoid legal issues. Here are some strategies and specific language that you can use to protect yourself and ensure you're not crossing the line into providing medical care:

1. Disclaimers
Clearly state that your services are educational or coaching in nature, and not intended as a substitute for professional medical advice, diagnosis, or treatment. Use disclaimers prominently on your website, in written materials, and at the beginning of your sessions. Example disclaimer:

"Disclaimer: The information provided during this session is for educational and informational purposes only and does not constitute medical advice or a patient-provider relationship. Please consult your healthcare provider before making any changes to your health or exercise routine."
2. Language to Avoid & Alternative Phrasing
Avoid language that implies direct care or treatment. Refrain from using terms like "treatment," "prescription," "therapy," or "diagnosis." Instead, use terms like:

"General guidance," "educational information," "suggestions," "best practices," or "insights."
For example:

Instead of: "I recommend you perform these exercises."
Use: "These exercises are commonly used for people experiencing similar symptoms, but please discuss this with your healthcare provider to determine if they're right for you."
3. Clarify Your Role
Make it clear that you are acting as a coach or educator, not a healthcare provider in this context. Examples:

"I am providing this information as a coach/educator, not as a healthcare provider."
"Our interaction today is intended to provide general information and education, and does not establish a clinical relationship."
4. Encourage Seeking Medical Advice
Always direct individuals to seek personal medical advice from their healthcare provider for specific questions or issues:

"Please consult with your physician or healthcare provider before implementing any changes or if you have specific concerns about your condition."
5. Avoid Providing Individualized Treatment Plans
Avoid crafting specific treatment plans or exercises tailored to an individualâ€™s specific condition without an assessment, as this could be interpreted as practicing physical therapy. Instead, focus on providing generalized information:

Instead of: "Based on your symptoms, you should do these exercises."
Use: "These are some common exercises that people find helpful for this type of issue. Please discuss them with your provider to determine if they are suitable for you."
6. Document Everything
If you are operating online, maintain records of any interactions and ensure that disclaimers are present in emails, websites, or any digital communications. This documentation can be valuable in showing your intent and the nature of the relationship.

7. Terms of Use Agreement
If you have a website or provide online courses, create a Terms of Use Agreement that participants must accept. This should outline:

The nature of the relationship (educational vs. therapeutic).
Clear disclaimers of liability.
Limitations of your services.
8. Consent for Coaching Sessions
If you provide one-on-one coaching, get explicit consent from the individual, acknowledging that:

"This session is for educational purposes only, and no patient-clinician relationship is being established."
Having them sign a consent form helps protect both parties and establishes boundaries.

9. Understand Scope of Practice Laws
Different states or regions have different laws about what is allowed without a license. Make sure to review local regulations regarding your professional capacity, especially as a physical therapist. For example, the use of certain titles (like "Physical Therapist") can imply a clinical relationship, which could create liability issues. Instead, you could use titles such as "Health Coach" or "Wellness Educator" for clarity.

These measures can help ensure you stay within the bounds of providing coaching or educational services without crossing into clinical practice, reducing legal risks while still providing value to your audience.

> **Response 2:** Yea you need to be clear and have a waiver or disclaimer, but the question is â€œwill it stand up in court?â€Anyone wanting to sue you will go for your highest license because it means a bigger settlement. I would look into consulting insurance from a local commercial insurance rep to protect you there. PTs absolutely consult, but if you are educating individually on how to help themselves, itâ€™s a blurry line (vs assisting with a companyâ€™s injury prevention plan, or teaching a class).

Hire a good lawyer, make it clear you arenâ€™t providing treatment (not a bad idea to have documentation for sessions to show you didnâ€™t provide treatment), but itâ€™s tough. Like imagine a surgeon opening a nutrition business and then a person goes into diabetic shock.  You better believe that lawyer is going after his medical license. If the surgeon documented what happened each session showing he didnâ€™t advise sugar and the patient signed a waiver acknowledging it wasnâ€™t medical advice then at least the surgeon has a chance in court.

> **Response 2:** I transitioned from 5 years of acute care and 3 years in geriatric to go back to more traditional outpatient and sports. 
First of you have to remember that as a PT youâ€™ve probably forgotten more about this stuff than most people will know in their entire lives, so youâ€™re very qualified even if out of practice! Biggest thing was just brushing up on special testing, reviewing some courses on conditions that you might feel a little rusty on. 
Overall itâ€™s mainly the flow of evals and treatments that takes a little time to get back into. But youâ€™ll be fine and your background in acute or inpatient is a huge huge huge bonus when working with folks who have been through it or when seeing more medically complex patients. Itâ€™s a strength not a liability!

### Discussion 728: 

**Date:** 2022-05-14 12:18:20 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 0 reactions, 3 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/966836587420907/

**Original Post:**
Clinical question: has anyone treated with a "slimmers neuropathy" before?

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** I have not, nor had I heard of the diagnosis before this post. Did a brief pubmed literature search and it sounds like thiamine deficiency from poor nutrition with excessive weight loss was a big component. Sounds like you'd treat it like any other peroneal nerve dysfunction. Orthotics/braces if foot drop is severe enough. Russian estim maybe. Stretching to avoid contractures if nerve damage is that severe. Gait retraining and foot/ankle strengthening. I didn't read anything about prognosis tho. I wonder if supplementation of thiamine helps? Or if its permanent... not sure.

### Discussion 729:

**Original Post:**
I recently took a dry needling class in atlanta and I practice in Florida. Can i start adding DN in my treatments or do i have to be supervised  every time I dry needle until I get certified or take additional courses ?

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** In FL you have to be certified and then have 25 supervised sessions.

> **Response 2:** Certification first before you can needle as a treatment. Both UE/ LE courses will get the contact hour requirements. THEN itâ€™s 25 treatments under supervision. Then you can submit for the DN tie to your license number. Once you get the confirmation that your license is updated, then you can needle independently.

### Discussion 754: 

**Date:** 2023-04-14 00:23:23 | **Topic:** 07-clinical-practice-specialties
**Engagement:** 1 reactions, 2 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1221441295293767/

**Original Post:**
Anyone have experience with TENEX for lateral epicondylitis? Good success? Healing time? I have a patient that is asking me about it. Thank you!

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** I did prolotherapy and it worked great. Better than surgery and down time. TENEX is supposed to work well.

> **Response 2:** If the diagnosis is accurate and the person has observable tendinosis at the extensor group on the lateral epicondyle it can be helpful.  Rehab is longer if there is hyperemia on US.  If the person has a tear within the capsule of the RC joint then this will only partially alter their pain.

If youâ€™ve done dry needling to the region that is planned for the procedure several times (especially with periosteal pecking) and they havenâ€™t noticed any changes then this procedure has a lower likelihood of changing things since your needles couldnâ€™t affect the pain generator.

---

### Discussion 763: 

**Date:** 2024-05-01 03:22:25 | **Topic:** 14-continuing-education-ceus
**Engagement:** 1 reactions, 2 comments
**Source:** https://www.facebook.com/groups/uncagedcliniciangroup/permalink/1461295407975020/

**Original Post:**
I've been thinking about branching out recently as I've noticed a need for mobile Parkinson's style programming like RockSteady or LSVT in my area. What would be first steps for this outside of gaining the certification/clearance to use the programming systems? 

Also, if it's the programming/groups that's being provided would it HAVE to be called OT and require an order/be billed as OT? Or is there a way around the referral process to make it more accessible? I live in Illinois and am fairly certain it's a direct access state per the state practice act but I could have misread. Ideally, I imagine running it like a gym model - offering groups and 1:1 at a specific "class rate" or "membership rate."

Thanks for any and all insight!!

**Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** Honestly Iâ€™d look into PWR instead of LSVT- itâ€™s more adaptable and modifiable to patients. I know a lot of personal trainers teach PWR classesâ€¦community word of mouth, no formal referrals

> **Response 2:** In order to officially offer PWR, LSVT BIG, or Rock Steady, you would need to take the respective courses to get certified.

To offer PWR as a non-therapist, you could get certified as an instructor. That track is geared toward the various fitness instructor roles, and can of course also be taken by clinicians.

Rock Steady is by default a wellness/exercise based certification. You can get certified as a therapist and could use the ideas in your practice, but you would not be providing the skills of your discipline. It would be a Rock Steady class. 

As far as I know, there are only clinician certifications for LSVT BIG. There is BIG for Life, though, which may be something other fitness instructors qualify to get certified in. I frankly haven't seen many of those classes, and there's a prerequisite for the patient/client to have already gone through and graduated from a full round of LSVT BIG.

...

I am certified in both PWR and LSVT BIG as a clinician. I have a mobile therapy model and rarely use LSVT BIG because it's exhausting to me personally, and because most of my clients would struggle with the intensity due to age, anxiety, orthopedic concerns, etc.

I plan to get certified as a PWR Moves Instructor, which is way more focused on building an exercise class. As a therapist, I highly value both PWR certification tracks. (I might also get a personal training cert.)

Keep in mind that if you do mobile THERAPY visits, most of these folks have Medicare or a Medicare replacement plan with out of network benefits. Unless you only want to work with YOPD or only want to do exercise classes, I strongly recommend you examine the rules for Medicare billing. The Medicare Billing for (Mostly) Cash Based PT, OT, SLP Providers Group can help with that area.

---

**Original Post:**
Creating a continuing education course  and I have questions!

On follow-up, he tolerated the session well and felt good afterward, but I see a lack of structure and consistency with his home exercise program, which I think is contributing to ongoing decline.

Would you recommend a different approach? If so what kind of approach and how do you community this to the family. The family is really worried about the patient and because of a lack of â€œgoods insuranceâ€ the patient is not getting the proper care from his medical team. **Community Responses:**

> **Response 1:** This sounds tough. I would probably approach as safety training and how to use the proper equipment to prevent falls. Once CGs and pt does that with less falls, I would potentially even DC if medical situation fluctuates too much. Sometimes I frame it as saving their PT visits for when we can make more progress and for when theyâ€™ll really benefit it. If their medical condition prevents progress, itâ€™s also really hard to justify on  a note and can risk insurance denial. I straight up tell the family how insurance works and that we need to show progress and medically necessary treatment and respond to continue. Iâ€™ll be firm but kind is saying more than 1x/ week or every other week will not be justifiable with current condition but when their condition improves, we can try increased frequency again..
